prologue _ chapter i _ introductory there be no rule for write a play it be easy indeed to lay down negative recommendation -- to instruct the beginner how _ not _ to do it but most of these do n't be rather obvious and those which be not obvious be apt to be questionable it be certain for instance that if you want you play to be act anywhere else than in china you must not plan it in sixteen act of a hour apiece but where be the tyro who need a text-book to tell he that on the other hand most theorist of to-day would make it a axiom that you must not let you character narrate they circumstance or expound they motive in speech address either directly to the audience or ostensibly to they solitary self but when we remember that of all dramatic opening there be none finer than that which show richard plantagenet limp down the empty stage to say -- now be the winter of we discontent make glorious summer by this sun of york and all the cloud that lour would upon we house in the deep bosom of the ocean bury -- we feel that the axiom require large qualification there be no absolute rule in fact except such as be dictate by the plainest common sense aristotle himself do not so much dogmatize as analyze classify and generalize from the practice of the attic dramatist he say you have better rather than you must it be horace in a age of deep dramatic decadence who re-state the pseudo-aristotelian formula of the alexandrian as though they be unassailable dogma of art how come it then that there be a constant demand for text-book of the art and craft of drama how come it that so many people -- and i among the number -- who could not write a play to save they life be eager to tell other how to do so and stranger still how come it that so many people be willing to sit at the foot of these instructor it be not so with the novel popular as be that form of literature guide to novel-writing if they exist at all be comparatively rare why be people possess with the idea that the art of dramatic fiction differ from that of narrative fiction in that it can and must be teach the reason be clear and be so far valid as to excuse if not to justify such work as the present the novel as soon as it be legibly write exist for what it be worth the page of black and white be the sole intermediary between the creative and the perceptive brain even the act of printing merely widen the possible appeal : it do not alter its nature but the drama before it can make its proper appeal at all must be run through a highly complex piece of mechanism -- the theater -- the precise condition of which be to most beginner a fascinating mystery while they feel a strong inward conviction of they ability to master it they be possess with a idea often exaggerated and superstitious of its technical complexity have as a rule little or no opportunity of closely examine or experiment with it they be eager to read it up as they might any other machine that be the case of the average aspirant who have neither the instinct of the theater fully develop in he blood nor such a congenital lack of that instinct as to be wholly inapprehensive of any technical difficulty or problem the intelligent novice standing between these extreme tend as a rule to overrate the efficacy of theoretical instruction and to expect of analytic criticism more than it have to give there be thus a fine opening for pedantry on the one side and quackery on the other to rush in the pedant in this context be he who construct a set of rule from metaphysical or psychological first principle and profess to bring down a dramatic decalogue from the sinaus of some lecture-room in the university of weissnichtwo the quack on the other hand be he who generalize from the worst practice of the most vulgar theatrical journeyman and have no higher ambition than to interpret the oracle of the box-office if he succeed in so do he function would not be wholly despicable but as he be generally devoid of insight and as moreover the oracle of the box-office vary from season to season if not from month to month he lucubration be about as valuable as those of zadkiel or old moore -lrb- 1 -rrb- what then be the excuse for such a discussion as be here attempt have admit that there be no rule for dramatic composition and that the quest of such rule be apt to result either in pedantry or quackery why should i myself set forth upon so fruitless and foolhardy a enterprise it be precisely because i be alive to its danger that i have some hope of avoid they rule there be none but it do not follow that some of the thousand who be fascinate by the art of the playwright may not profit by have they attention call in a plain and practical way to some of its problem and possibility i have myself feel the need of some such handbook when would-be dramatist have come to I for advice and guidance it be easy to name excellent treatise on the drama but the aim of such book be to guide the judgment of the critic rather than the creative impulse of the playwright there be also valuable collection of dramatic criticism but any practical hint that they may contain be scatter and unsystematic on the other hand the advice one be apt to give to beginner -- go to the theater study its condition and mechanism for yourself -- be in fact of very doubtful value it might in many case be wiser to warn the aspirant to keep himself unspotted from the playhouse to send he there be to imperil on the one hand he originality of vision on the other he individuality of method he may fall under the influence of some great master and see life only through he eye or he may become so habituate to the current trick of the theatrical trade as to lose all sense of they conventionality and falsity and find himself in the end better fit to write what i have call a quack handbook than a living play it would be ridiculous of course to urge a aspirant positively to avoid the theater but the common advice to steep himself in it be beset with danger it may be ask why if i have any guidance and help to give i do not take it myself and write play instead of instruct other in the art this be a variant of a ancient and fallacious jibe against criticism in general it be quite true that almost all critic who be worth they salt be stickit artist assuredly if i have the power i should write play instead of write about they but one may have a great love for a art and some insight into its principle and method without the innate faculty require for actual production on the other hand there be nothing to show that if i be a creative artist i should be a good mentor for beginner a accomplished painter may be the best teacher of painter but a accomplished dramatist be scarcely the best guide for dramatist he can not analyze he own practice and discriminate between that in it which be of universal validity and that which may be good for he but would be bad for any one else if he happen to be a great man he would inevitably even if unconsciously seek to impose upon he disciple he individual attitude towards life if he be a lesser man he would teach they only he trick but dramatist do not as a matter of fact take pupil or write handbook -lrb- 2 -rrb- when they expound they principle of art it be generally in answer to or in anticipation of criticism -- with a view in short not to help other but to defend themselves if beginner then be to find any systematic guidance they must turn to the critic not to the dramatist and no person of common sense hold it a reproach to a critic to tell he that he be a stickit playwright if question be worth discuss at all they be worth discuss gravely when in the follow page i be find treat with all solemnity matter of apparently trivial detail i beg the reader to believe that very possibly i do not in my heart overrate they importance one thing be certain and must be emphasize from the outset : namely that if any part of the dramatist 's art can be teach it be only a comparatively mechanical and formal part -- the art of structure one may learn how to tell a story in good dramatic form : how to develop and marshal it in such a way as best to seize and retain the interest of a theatrical audience but no teaching or study can enable a man to choose or invent a good story and much less to do that which alone lend dignity to dramatic story-telling -- to observe and portray human character this be the aim and end of all serious drama and it will be apt to appear as though in the follow page this aim and end be ignore in reality it be not so if i hold comparatively mechanical question of pure craftsmanship to be worth discuss it be because i believe that only by aid of competent craftsmanship can the greatest genius enable he creation to live and breathe upon the stage the profoundest insight into human nature and destiny can not find valid expression through the medium of the theater without some understanding of the peculiar art of dramatic construction some people be bear with such a instinct for this art that a very little practice render they master of it some people be bear with a hollow in they cranium where the bump of drama ought to be but between these extreme as i say before there be many people with moderately develop and cultivable faculty and it be these who i trust may find some profit in the follow discussion -lrb- 3 -rrb- let they not forget however that the topic treat of be merely the indispensable rudiment of the art and be not for a moment to be mistake for its ultimate and incommunicable secret beethoven could not have compose the ninth symphony without a mastery of harmony and counterpoint but there be thousand of master of harmony and counterpoint who could not compose the ninth symphony the art of theatrical story-telling be necessarily relative to the audience to whom the story be to be tell one must assume a audience of a certain status and characteristic before one can rationally discuss the best method of appeal to its intelligence and its sympathy the audience i have throughout assume be draw from what may be call the ordinary educate public of london and new york it be not a ideal or a specially select audience but it be somewhat above the average of the theatre-going public that average be sadly pull down by the myriad frequenter of musical farce and absolutely worthless melodrama it be such a audience as assemble every night at say the half-dozen best theater of each city a peculiarly intellectual audience it certainly be not i gladly admit that theatrical art owe much in both country to voluntary organization of intelligent or would-be intelligent -lrb- 4 -rrb- playgoer who have combine to provide themselves with form of drama which specially interest they and do not attract the great public but i be entirely convince that the drama renounce its chief privilege and glory when it waive its claim to be a popular art and be content to address itself to coterie however high-browed shakespeare do not write for a coterie : yet he produce some work of considerable subtlety and profundity molière be popular with the ordinary parterre of he day : yet he play have endure for over two century and the end of they vitality do not seem to be in sight ibsen do not write for a coterie though special and regrettable circumstance have make he in england something of a coterie-poet in scandinavium in germany even in america he cast he spell over great audience if not through long run -lrb- which be a vice of the merely commercial theater -rrb- at any rate through frequently-repeated representation so far as i know history record no instance of a playwright fail to gain the ear of he contemporary and then be recognize and appreciate by posterity alfr de musset might perhaps be cite as a case in point but he do not write with a view to the stage and make no bid for contemporary popularity as soon as it occur to people to produce he play they be find to be delightful let no playwright then make it he boast that he can not disburden he soul within the three hour ' limit and can not produce play intelligible or endurable to any audience but a band of adept a popular audience however do not necessarily mean the mere riff-raff of the theatrical public there be a large class of playgoer both in england and america which be capable of appreciate work of a high intellectual order if only it do not ignore the fundamental condition of theatrical presentation it be a audience of this class that i have in mind throughout the follow page and i believe that a playwright who despise such a audience will do so to the detriment not only of he popularity and profit but of the artistic quality of he work some people may exclaim : why should the dramatist concern himself about he audience that may be all very well for the mere journeyman of the theater the hack who write to a actor-manager 's order -- not for the true artist he have a soul above all such petty consideration art to he be simply self-expression he write to please himself and have no thought of currying favor with a audience whether intellectual or idiotic to this i reply simply that to a artist of this way of think i have nothing to say he have a perfect right to express himself in a whole literature of so-called play which may possibly be study and even act by society organize to that laudable end but the dramatist who declare he end to be mere self-expression stultify himself in that very phrase the painter may paint the sculptor model the lyric poet sing simply to please himself -lrb- 5 -rrb- but the drama have no meaning except in relation to a audience it be a portrayal of life by means of a mechanism so devise as to bring it home to a considerable number of people assemble in a give place the public it have be well say constitute the theater the moment a playwright confines he work within the two or three hour ' limit prescribe by western custom for a theatrical performance he be currying favor with a audience that limit be impose simply by the physical endurance and power of sustained attention that can be demand of western human being assemble in a theater doubtless a author could express himself more fully and more subtly if he ignore these limitation the moment he submit to they he renounce the pretence that mere self-expression be he aim i know that there be haughty-soul who make no such submission and express themselves in drama which so far as they proportion be concerned might as well be epic poem or historical romance -lrb- 6 -rrb- to they i repeat i have nothing to say the one and only subject of the follow discussion be the best method of fitting a dramatic theme for representation before a audience assemble in a theater but this be it note do not necessarily mean write down to the audience in question it be by obey not by ignore the fundamental condition of he craft that the dramatist may hope to lead he audience upward to the highest intellectual level which he himself can attain these page in short be address to student of play-writing who sincerely desire to do sound artistic work under the condition and limitation of the actual living playhouse this do not mean of course that they ought always to be study what the public want the dramatist possibility that this might be interpret as a sneer at _ the dynast _ -- a great work by a great poet -rrb- _ chapter ii _ the choice of a theme the first step towards write a play be manifestly to choose a theme even this simple statement however require careful examination before we can grasp its full import what in the first place do we mean by a theme and secondly in what sense can we or ought we to choose one theme may mean either of two thing : either the subject of a play or its story the former be perhaps its proper or more convenient sense the theme of _ romeo and juliet _ be youthful love cross by ancestral hate the theme of _ othello _ be jealousy the theme of _ le tartufe _ be hypocrisy the theme of _ caste _ be fond heart and coronet the theme of _ get married _ be get marry the theme of _ maternité _ be maternity to every play it be possible at a pinch to assign a theme but in many play it be evident that no theme expressible in abstract term be present to the author 's mind nor be these always play of a low class it be only by a somewhat artificial process of abstraction that we can formulate a theme for _ as you like it _ for _ the way of the world _ or for _ hedda gabler _ the question now arise : ought a theme in its abstract form to be the first germ of a play ought the dramatist to say go to i will write a play on temperance or on woman 's suffrage or on capital and labor and then cast about for a story to illustrate he theme this be a possible but not a promising method of procedure a story make to the order of a moral concept be always apt to advertise its origin to the detriment of its illusive quality if a play be to be a moral apologue at all it be well to say so frankly -- probably in the title -- and aim not at verisimilitude but at neatness and appositeness in the work out of the fable the french _ proverbe _ proceeds on this principle and be often very witty and charming -lrb- 1 -rrb- a good example in english be _ a pair of spectacles _ by mr sydney grundy found on a play by labiche in this bright little comedy every incident and situation bear upon the general theme and please we not by its probability but by its ingenious appropriateness the dramatic fable in fact hold very much the same rank in drama as the narrative fable hold in literature at large we take pleasure in they on condition that they be witty and that they do not pretend to be what they be not a play manifestly suggest by a theme of temporary interest will often have a great but no less temporary success for instance though there be a good deal of clever character-drawing in _ a englishman 's home _ by major du maurier the theme be so evidently the source and inspiration of the play that it will scarcely bear revival in america where the theme be of no interest the play fail it be possible no doubt to name excellent play in which the theme in all probability precede both the story and the character in the author 's mind such play be most of m brieux 's such play be mr galsworthy 's _ strife _ and _ justice _ the french play in my judgment suffer artistically from the obtrusive predominance of the theme -- that be to say the abstract element -- over the human and concrete factor in the composition mr galsworthy 's more delicate and unemphatic art elude this danger at any rate in _ strife _ we do not remember until all be over that he character represent class and he action be one might almost say a sociological symbol if then the theme do as a matter of fact come first in the author 's conception he will do well either to make it patently and confessedly dominant as in the _ proverbe _ or to take care that as in _ strife _ it be not suffer to make its domination feel except as a afterthought -lrb- 2 -rrb- no outside force should appear to control the free rhythm of the action the theme may sometimes be not a idea a abstraction or a principle but rather a environment a social phenomenon of one sort or another the author 's primary object in such a case be not to portray any individual character or tell any definite story but to transfer to the stage a animated picture of some broad aspect or phase of life without concentrate the interest on any one figure or group there be theorist who would by definition exclude from the domain of drama any such cinematograph-play as they would probably call it but we shall see cause as we go on to distrust definition especially when they seek to clothe themselves with the authority of law tableau-play of the type here in question may even claim classical precedent what else be ben jonson 's _ bartholomew fair _ what else be schiller 's _ wallenstein lager _ amongst more recent play hauptmann 's _ die weber _ and gorky 's _ nachtasyl _ be perhaps the best example of the type the drawback of such theme be not that they do not conform to this or that canon of art but that it need a exceptional amount of knowledge and dramaturgic skill to handle they successfully it be far easier to tell a story on the stage than to paint a picture and few playwright can resist the temptation to foist a story upon they picture thus mar it by a inharmonious intrusion of melodrama or farce this have often be do upon deliberate theory in the belief that no play can exist or can attract playgoer without a definite and more or less exciting plot thus the late jame a herne insert into a charming idyllic picture of rural life entitle _ shore acre _ a melodramatic scene in a lighthouse which be hopelessly out of key with the rest of the play the dramatist who know any particular phase of life so thoroughly as to be able to transfer its characteristic incident to the stage may be advise to defy both critical and managerial prejudice and give he tableau-play just so much of story as may naturally and inevitably fall within its limit one of the most admirable and enthralling scene i ever see on any stage be that of the trafalgar square suffrage meeting in miss elizabeth robin 's _ vote for woman _ throughout a whole act it hold we spellbound while the story of the play stand still and we forget its existence it be only within a few minute of the end when the story be drag in neck and crop that the reality of the thing vanish and the interest with it \* \* \* \* \* if a abstract theme be not a advisable starting-point what be a character a situation or a story on this point it would be absurd to lay down any rule the more so as in many case a playwright be quite unable to say in what form the germ of a play first float into he mind the suggestion may come from a newspaper paragraph from a incident see in the street from a emotional adventure or a comic misadventure from a chance word drop by a acquaintance or from some flotsam or jetsam of phrase or fable that have drift from the other end of history often too the original germ whatever it may be be transform beyond recognition before a play be do -lrb- 3 -rrb- in the mind of the playwright fig grow from thistle and a silk purse -- perhaps a fortunatus ' purse -- may often be make from a sow 's ear the whole delicate texture of ibsen 's _ doll 's house _ be weave from a commonplace story of a woman who forge a cheque in order to redecorate she drawing-room stevenson 's romance of _ prince otto _ -lrb- to take a example from fiction -rrb- grow out of a tragedy on the subject of semiramis one thing however we may say with tolerable confidence : whatever may be the germ of a play -- whether it be a anecdote a situation or what not -- the play will be of small account as a work of art unless character at a very early point enter into and condition its development the story which be independent of character -- which can be carry through by a give number of ready-made puppet -- be essentially a trivial thing unless at a early stage of the organize process character begin to take the upper hand -- unless the playwright find himself think oh yes george be just the man to do this or that be quite foreign to jane 's temperament -- he may be pretty sure that it be a piece of mechanism he be put together not a drama with flesh and blood in it the difference between a live play and a dead one be that in the former the character control the plot while in the latter the plot control the character which be not to say of course that there may not be clever and entertaining play which be dead in this sense and dull and unattractive play which be live a great deal of ink have be waste in controversy over a remark of aristotle 's that the action or _ mutho _ not the character or _ êthos _ be the essential element in drama the statement be absolutely true and wholly unimportant a play can exist without anything that can be call character but not without some sort of action this be imply in the very word drama which mean a do not a mere say or exist it would be possible no doubt to place don quixote or falstaff or peer gynt on the stage and let he develop he character in mere conversation or even monologue without ever move from he chair but it be a truism that deed not word be the demonstration and test of character wherefore from time immemorial it have be the recognize business of the theater to exhibit character in action historically too we find that drama have everywhere originate in the portrayal of a action -- some exploit or some calamity in the career of some demigod or hero thus story or plot be by definition tradition and practical reason the fundamental element in drama but do it therefore follow that it be the noblest element or that by which its value should be measure assuredly not the skeleton be in a sense the fundamental element in the human organism it can exist and with a little assistance retain its form when strip of muscle and blood and nerve whereas a boneless man would be a amorphous heap more helpless than a jelly-fish but do we therefore account the skeleton man 's noblest part scarcely it be by he blood and nerve that he live not by he bone and it be because he bone be comparatively speak dead matter that they continue to exist when the flesh have fall away from they it be therefore if not a misreading of aristotle -lrb- 4 -rrb- at any rate a perversion of reason to maintain that the drama live by action rather than by character action ought to exist for the sake of character : when the relation be reverse the play may be a ingenious toy but scarcely a vital work of art \* \* \* \* \* it be time now to consider just what we mean when we say that the first step towards play-writing be the choice of a theme in many case no doubt it be the plain and literal fact that the impulse to write some play -- any play -- exist so to speak in the abstract unassociated with any particular subject and that the would-be playwright proceeds as he think to set he imagination to work and invent a story but this frame of mind be to be regard with suspicion few play of much value one may guess have result from such a abstract impulse invention in these case be apt to be nothing but recollection in disguise the shake of a kaleidoscope form of fragmentary reminiscence i remember once in some momentary access of ambition try to invent a play i occupy several hour of a long country walk in as i believe create out of nothing at all a dramatic story when at last i have model it into some sort of coherency i step back from it in my mind as it be and contemplate it as a whole no sooner have i do so than it begin to seem vaguely familiar where have i see this story before i ask myself and it be only after cudgel my brain for several minute that i find i have re-invent ibsen 's _ hedda gabler _ thus when we think we be choose a plot out of the void we be very apt to be in fact ransack the store-house of memory the plot which choose we be much more to be depend upon -- the idea which come when we least expect it perhaps from the most unlikely quarter clamor at the gate of birth and will not let we rest till it be clothe in dramatic flesh and blood -lrb- 5 -rrb- it may very well happen of course that it have to wait -- that it have to be pigeon-hole for a time until its due turn come -lrb- 6 -rrb- occasionally perhaps it may slip out of its pigeon-hole for a air only to be put back again in a slightly more developed form then at last its convenient season will arrive and the play will be work out write and launch into the struggle for life in the sense of select from among a number of embryonic theme store in he mind the playwright have often to make a deliberate choice but when move by a purely abstract impulse he go out of set purpose to look for a theme it may be doubt whether he be likely to return with any very valuable treasure-trove -lrb- 7 -rrb- the same principle hold good in the case of the ready-made poetic or historical theme which be -- rightly or wrongly -- consider suitable for treatment in blank verse whether and how far the blank verse drama can nowadays be regard as a vital and viable form be a question to be consider later in the meantime it be sufficient to say that whatever principle of conception and construction apply to the modern prose drama apply with equal cogency to the poetic drama the verse-poet may perhaps take one or two licens deny to the prose-poet for instance we may find reason to think the soliloquy more excusable in verse than in prose but fundamentally the two form be rule by the same set of condition which the verse-poet no less than the prose-poet can ignore only at he peril unless indeed he renounce from the outset all thought of the stage and choose to produce that cumbrous nondescript a closet drama of such we do not speak but glance and pass on what law indeed can apply to a form which have no proper element but like the amphibious animal describe by the sailor can not live on land and die in the water to return to we immediate topic the poet who essay dramatic composition on mere abstract impulse because other poet have do so or because he be tell that it pay be only too likely to produce willy-nilly a closet drama let he beware of say to himself i will gird up my loin and write a play shall it be a phaedra or a semiramis or a sappho or a cleopatra a julian or a attilum or a consider for a little what we mean when we use the term dramatic we shall probably not arrive at any definition which can be apply as a infallible touchstone to distinguish the dramatic from the undramatic perhaps indeed the upshot may rather be to place the student on he guard against trouble too much about the formal definition of critical theorist the orthodox opinion of the present time be that which be generally associate with the name of the late ferdinand brunetière the theater in general say that critic be nothing but the place for the development of the human will attack the obstacle oppose to it by destiny fortune or circumstance and again : drama be a representation of the will of man in conflict with the mysterious power or natural force which limit and belittle we it be one of we throw live upon the stage there to struggle against fatality against social law against one of he fellow-mortal against himself if need be against the ambition the interest the prejudice the folly the malevolence of those who surround he -lrb- 1 -rrb- the difficulty about this definition be that while it describe the matter of a good many drama it do not lay down any true differentium -- any characteristic common to all drama and possess by no other form of fiction many of the greatest play in the world can with difficulty be bring under the formulum while the majority of romance and other story come under it with ease where for instance be the struggle in the _ agamemnon _ there be no more struggle between clytemnestra and agamemnon than there be between the spider and the fly who walk into he net there be not even a struggle in clytemnestra 's mind agamemnon 's doom be seal from the outset and she merely carry out a pre-arranged plot there be contest indeed in the succeed play of the trilogy but it will scarcely be argue that the _ agamemnon _ take alone be not a great drama even the _ oedipus _ of sophocle though it may at first sight seem a typical instance of a struggle against destiny do not really come under the definition oedipus in fact do not struggle at all he struggle in so far as that word can be apply to he misguided effort to escape from the toil of fate be all thing of the past in the actual course of the tragedy he simply writhe under one revelation after another of bygone error and unwitting crime it would be a mere play upon word to recognize as a dramatic struggle the writhe of a worm on a hook and do not this description apply very closely to the part play by another great protagonist -- othello to wit there be no struggle no conflict between he and iago it be iago alone who exert any will neither othello nor desdemona make the smallest fight from the moment when iago set he machination to work they be like people slide down a ice-slope to a inevitable abyss where be the conflict in _ as you like it _ no one surely will pretend that any part of the interest or charm of the play arise from the struggle between the banish duke and the usurper or between orlando and oliver there be not even the conflict if so it can be call which nominally bring so many hundred of play under the brunetière canon -- the conflict between a eager lover and a more or less reluctant maid or take again ibsen 's _ ghost _ -- in what valid sense can it be say that that tragedy show we will struggle against obstacle oswald doubtless wish to live and he mother desire that he should live but this mere will for life can not be the differentium that make of _ ghost _ a drama if the reluctant descent of the downward path to death constitute drama then tolstoy 's _ death of ivan ilytch _ would be one of the greatest drama ever write -- which it certainly be not yet again if we want to see will struggle against obstacle the classic to turn to be not _ hamlet _ not _ lear _ but _ robinson crusoe _ yet no one except a pantomime librettist ever see a drama in defoe 's narrative in a platonic dialogue in _ paradise lose _ in _ john gilpin _ there be a struggle of will against obstacle there be none in _ hannele _ which nevertheless be a deeply-moving drama such a struggle be characteristic of all great fiction from _ clarissa harlowe _ to _ the house with the green shutter _ whereas in many play the struggle if there be any at all be the merest matter of form -lrb- for instance a quite conventional love-story -rrb- while the real interest reside in something quite different the plain truth seem to be that conflict be _ one _ of the most dramatic element in life and that many drama -- perhaps most -- do as a matter of fact turn upon strife of one sort or another but it be clearly a error to make conflict indispensable to drama and especially to insist -- as do some of brunetière 's follower -- that the conflict must be between will and will a stand-up fight between will and will -- such a fight as occur in say the _ hippolytus _ of euripide or racine 's _ andromaque _ or molière 's _ tartufe _ or ibsen 's _ pretender _ or duma 's _ françillon _ or sudermann 's _ heimat _ or sir arthur pinero 's _ gay lord quex _ or mr shaw 's _ candida _ or mr galsworthy 's _ strife _ -- such a stand-up fight i say be no doubt one of the intensest form of drama but it be comparatively rare at any rate as the formulum of a whole play in individual scene a conflict of will be frequent enough but it be after all only one among a multitude of equally tell form of drama no one can say that the balcony scene in _ romeo and juliet _ be undramatic or the galeoto fú il libro scene in mr stephen phillip 's _ paolo and francesca _ yet the point of these scene be not a clash but a ecstatic concordance of will be the death-scene of cleopatra undramatic or the banquet scene in _ macbeth _ or the pastoral act in _ the winter 's tale _ yet in none of these be there any conflict of will in the whole range of drama there be scarcely a passage which one would call more specifically dramatic than the screen scene in _ the school for scandal _ yet it would be the veriest quibble to argue that any appreciable part of its effect arise from the clash of will against will this whole comedy indeed suffice to show the emptiness of the theory with a little strain it be possible to bring it within the letter of the formulum but who can pretend that any considerable part of the attraction or interest of the play be due to that possibility the champion of the theory moreover place it on a metaphysical basis finding in the will the essence of human personality and therefore of the art which show human personality raise to its highest power it seem unnecessary however to apply to schopenhauer for a explanation of whatever validity the theory may possess for a sufficient account of the matter we need go no further than the simple psychological observation that human nature love a fight whether it be with club or with sword with tongue or with brain one of the earliest form of mediaeval drama be the estrif or flyting -- the scolding-match between husband and wife or between two rustic gossip this motive be glorified in the quarrel between brutus and cassius degrade in the patter of two knockabout comedian certainly there be nothing more tell in drama than a piece of cut-and-thrust dialogue after the fashion of the ancient stichomythium when a whole theme involve conflict or even a single scene of the nature describe as a passage-at-arm come naturally in the playwright 's way by all means let he seize the opportunity but do not let he reject a theme or scene as undramatic merely because it have no room for a clash of war will there be a variant of the conflict theory which underline the word obstacle in the above-quoted dictum of brunetière and lay down the rule : no obstacle no drama though far from be universally valid this form of the theory have a certain practical usefulness and may well be bear in mind many a play would have remain unwritten if the author have ask himself be there a sufficient obstacle between my two lover or in more general term between my character and the realization of they will there be nothing more futile than a play in which we feel that there be no real obstacle to the inevitable happy ending and that the curtain might just as well fall in the middle of the first act as at the end of the third comedy abound -lrb- though they reach its means we have pass in rapid survey the practice of shakespeare and ibsen in respect of they point and method of attack upon they theme what practical lesson can we now deduce from this examination one thing be clear : namely that there be no inherent superiority in one method over another there be masterpiece in which the whole crisis fall within the frame of the picture and masterpiece in which the greater part of the crisis have to be convey to we in retrospect only the catastrophe be transact before we eye genius can manifest itself equally in either form but each form have its peculiar advantage you can not in a retrospective play like _ rosmersholm _ attain anything like the magnificent onward rush of othello which move -- like to the pontick sea whose icy current and compulsive course ne'er feel retire ebb but keep due on to the propontick and the hellespont the movement of _ rosmersholm _ be rather like that of a wind river which flow with a full and steady current but seem sometimes to be almost retrace its course if then you aim at rapidity of movement you will choose a theme which leave little or nothing to retrospect and conversely if you have a theme the whole of which fall easily and conveniently within the frame of the picture you will probably take advantage of the fact to give you play animated and rapid movement there be a undeniable attraction in a play which constitute so to speak one brisk and continuous adventure begin develop and end before we eye for light comedy in particular be this a desirable form and for romantic play in which no very search character-study be attempt _ the tame of the shrew _ no doubt pass for a light comedy in shakespeare 's day though we describe it by a briefer name its rapid bustling action be possible because we be always ready to take the character of a shrew for grant it would have be a very different play have the poet require to account for katharine 's peculiarity of temper by a retrospective study of she heredity and upbringe many eighteenth-century comedy be single-adventure play or dual-adventure play in the sense that the main action sometimes stand aside to let a underplot take the stage both _ she stoop to conquer _ and _ the rival _ be good example of the rapid working-out of a intrigue engender develop and resolve all within the frame of the picture single-adventure play of a more modern type be the elder duma 's _ mademoiselle de belle-isle _ the younger duma 's _ francillon _ sardou 's _ divorçons _ sir arthur pinero 's _ gay lord quex _ mr shaw 's _ devil 's disciple _ oscar wilde 's _ importance of be earnest _ mr galsworthy 's _ silver box _ widely as these play differ in type and tone they be alike in this that they do not attempt to present very complex character-study or to probe the deep of human experience the last play cite _ the silver box _ may perhaps be think a exception to this rule but though the experience of the hapless charwoman be pitiful enough hers be a simple soul so inured to suffer that a little more or less be no such great matter the play be a admirable genre-picture rather than a search tragedy the point to be observe be that under modern condition it be difficult to produce a play of very complex psychological moral or emotional substance in which the whole crisis come within the frame of the picture the method of attack the crisis in the middle or towards the end be really a device for relax in some measure the narrow bound of theatrical representation and enable the playwright to deal with a larger segment of human experience it may be ask why modern condition should in this respect differ from elizabethan condition and why if shakespeare could produce such profound and complex tragedy as _ othello _ and _ king lear _ without a word of exposition or retrospect the modern dramatist should not go and do likewise the answer to this question be not simply that the modern dramatist be seldom a shakespeare that be true but we must look deeper than that there be in fact several point to be take into consideration for one thing -- this be a minor point -- shakespeare have really far more elbow-room than the playwright of to-day _ othello _ and _ king lear _ to say nothing of _ hamlet _ be exceedingly long play something like a third of they be omit in modern representation and when we speak of they richness and complexity of characterization we do not think simply of the play as we see they compress into act limit but of the play as we know they in the study it be possible no doubt for modern playwright to let themselves go in the matter of length and then print they play with bracket or other mark to show the passage omit in representation this be however essentially a inartistic practice and one can not regret that it have go out of fashion another point to be consider be this : be othello and lear really very complex character-study they be extremely vivid : they be project with enormous energy in action whose violence afford scope for the most vehement self-expression but be they not in reality colossally simple rather than complex it be true that in lear the phenomenon of insanity be reproduce with astonishing minuteness and truth but this do not imply any elaborate analysis or demand any great space hamlet be complex and be i talk for victory i should point out that _ hamlet _ be of all the tragedy precisely the one which do not come within the frame of the picture but the true secret of the matter do not lie here : it lie in the fact that hamlet unpack he heart to we in a series of soliloquy -- a device employ scarcely at all in the portrayal of othello and lear and deny to the modern dramatist -lrb- 1 -rrb- yet again the social position and environment of the great shakespearean character be take for grant no time be spend in place they in a give stratum of society or in establish they heredity tradition education to work rather in indirect suggestion than in direct expression he have in short to submit to a hundred hamper condition from which shakespeare be exempt wherefore even if he have shakespeare 's genius he would find it difficult to produce a very profound effect in a crisis work out from first to last before the eye of the audience nevertheless as before state such a crisis have a charm of its own there be a peculiar interest in watch the rise and development out of nothing as it be of a dramatic complication for this class of play -lrb- despite the shakespearean precedent -rrb- a quiet opening be often advisable rather than a strong _ einleitende akkord _ from calm through storm to calm be its characteristic formulum whether the conclude calm be one of life and serenity or of turn now to the _ oedipus _ -- i choose that play as a typical example of greek tragedy -- what sort of unity do we find it be the unity not of a continuous mass or mash but of carefully calculate five act and a epilogue it so happen that the greek theater do not possess a curtain and do possess a chorus consequently the greek dramatist employ the chorus as we employ the curtain to emphasize the successive stage of he action to mark the rhythm of its progress and incidentally to provide resting-place for the mind of the audience -- interval during which the strain upon they attention be relax or at any rate vary it be not even true that the greek habitually aim at such continuity of time as we find in _ get married _ they treat time ideally the imaginary duration of the story be as a even he however subject the event which take place behind the scene to a good deal of ideal compression of course when mr shaw protest that in _ get married _ he do not indulge in a deliberate display order and balance of clearly differentiate part which go to the structure of a greek tragedy a dramatist who can so develop he story as to bring it within the quasi-aristotelean unity perform a curious but not particularly difficult or valuable feat but this do not or ought not to imply the abandonment of the act-division which be no mere convention but a valuable means of mark the rhythm of the story when on the other hand you have no story to tell the act-division be manifestly superfluous but it need no virtuosity to dispense with it it be a grave error then to suppose that the act be a mere division of convenience impose by the limited power of attention of the human mind or by the need of the human body for occasional refreshment a play with a well-marked well-balanced act-structure be a higher artistic organism than a play with no act-structure just as a vertebrate animal be higher than a mollusc in every crisis of real life -lrb- unless it be so short as to be a mere incident -rrb- there be a rhythm of rise progress culmination and solution we be not always perhaps not often conscious of these stage but that be only because we do not reflect upon we experience while they be pass or map they out in memory when they be past we do however constantly apply to real-life crisis expression borrow more or less directly from the terminology of the drama we say somewhat incorrectly thing have come to a climax meaning thereby a culmination or we say the catastrophe be at hand or again what a fortunate _ dénouement _ be this as it may it be the business of the dramatist to analyze the crisis with which he deal and to present they to we in they rhythm of growth culmination solution to this end the act-division be -- not perhaps essential since the rhythm may be mark even in a one-act play -- but certainly of enormous and invaluable convenience si l'acte n'existait pas il faudrait l'inventer but as a matter of fact it have exist wherever in the western world the drama have develop beyond its rudest beginning it be doubtless the necessity for mark this rhythm that aristotle have in mind when he say that a dramatic action must have a begin a middle and a end take in its simplicity this principle would indicate the three-act division as the ideal scheme for a play as a matter of fact many of the best modern play in all language fall into three act one have only to note _ monsieur alphonse françillon la parisienne amoureuse a doll 's house ghost the master builder little eyolf johannisfeuer caste candida the benefit of the doubt the importance of be earnest the silver box _ and furthermore many old play which be nominally in five act really fall into a triple rhythm and might better have be divide into three alexandrian precept handed on by horace give to the five act division a purely arbitrary sanction which induce playwright to mask the natural rhythm of they theme beneath this artificial one -lrb- 2 -rrb- but in truth the three-act division ought no more to be elevated into a absolute rule than the five-act division we have see that a play consist or ought to consist of a great crisis work out through a series of minor crisis a act then ought to consist either of a minor crisis carry to its temporary solution or of a well-marked group of such crisis and there can be no rule as to the number of such crisis which ought to present themselves in the development of a given theme on the modern stage five act may be regard as the maximum simply by reason of the time-limit impose by social custom on a performance but one frequently see a melodrama divide into five act and eight tableau or even more which practically mean that the play be in eight or nine or ten act but that there will be only the four conventional interact in the course of the evening the playwright should not let himself be constrain by custom to force he theme into the arbitrary mould of a state number of act three act be a good number four act be a good number -lrb- 3 -rrb- there be no positive objection to five act should he find himself hanker after more act he will do well to consider whether he be not at one point or another fail in the art of condensation and trespassing on the domain of the novelist there be undoubted convenience in the rule of the modern stage : one act one scene a change of scene in the middle of a act be not only materially difficult but tend to impair the particular order of illusion at which the modern drama aim -lrb- 4 -rrb- roughly indeed a act may be define as any part of a give crisis which work itself out at one time and in one place but more fundamentally it be a segment of the action during which the author desire to hold the attention of he audience unbroken and unrelaxed it be no mere convention however which decree that the flight of time be best indicate by a interact when the curtain be down the action on the stage remain as it be in suspense the audience let its attention revert to the affair of real life and it be quite willing when the mimic world be once more reveal to suppose that any reasonable space of time have elapse while its thought be occupy with other matter it be much more difficult for it to accept a wholly imaginary lapse of time while its attention be centre on the mimic world some playwright have of late year adopt the device to the drawing-room sir arthur pinero employ this device with good effect in _ iris _ so do mr granville barker in _ waste _ and mr galsworthy in _ the silver box _ it be certainly far preferable to that ideal treatment of time which be common in the french drama of the nineteenth century and survive to this day in play adapt or imitate from the french i remember see in london not very long ago a one-act play on the subject of rouget de l'isle in the space of about half-an-hour he hand the manuscript of the marseillaise to a opera-singer whom he adore she take it away and sing it at the opera verisimilitude naturally and properly vary with the seriousness of the theme under treatment improbability be admissible in light comedy and still more in farce which would wreck the fortune of a drama purport to present a sober and faithful picture of real life act then mark the time-stage in the development of a give crisis and each act ought to embody a minor crisis of its own with a culmination and a temporary solution it would be no gain but a loss if a whole two hour ' or three hour ' action could be carry through in one continuous movement with no relaxation of the strain upon the attention of the audience and without a single point at which the spectator might review what be past and anticipate what be to come the act-division positively enhance the amount of pleasurable emotion through which the audience pass each act ought to stimulate and temporarily satisfy a interest of its own while definitely advance the main action the psychological principle be evident enough namely that there be more sensation to be get out of three or four comparatively brief experience suit to we power of perception than out of one protracted experience force on we without relief without contrast in such a way as to fatigue and deaden we faculty who would not rather drink three four or five glass of wine than put the bottle to he lip and let its contents pour down he throat in of the audience this may seem a tolerably obvious statement but it be worth while to examine a little more closely into its implication as to arouse the interest of the audience it be clear that very little specific advice can be give one can only say find a interesting theme state its preliminary clearly and crisply and let issue be join without too much delay there can be no rule for find a interesting theme any more than for catch the blue bird at a later stage we may perhaps attempt a summary enumeration of theme which be not interesting which have exhaust any interest they ever possess and repay careful avoidance but such a enumeration would be out of place here where we be study principle of form apart from detail of matter the arouse of interest however be one thing the carrying-forward of interest be another and on the latter point there be one or two thing that may profitably be say each act as we have see should consist of or at all event contain a subordinate crisis contributory to the main crisis of the play : and the art of act-construction lie in give to each act a individuality and interest of its own without so round it off as to obscure even for a moment its subsidiary and in the case of the first act its introductory relation to the whole this be a point which many dramatist ignore or undervalue very often when the curtain fall on a first or a second act one say this be a fairly good act in itself but whither do it lead what be to come of it all it awaken no definite anticipation and for two pin one would take up one 's hat and go home the author have neglect the art of carrying-forward the interest it be curious to note that in the most unsophisticated form of melodrama this art be deliberately ignore in play of the type of _ the worst woman in london _ it appear to be a absolute canon of art that every act must have a happy ending -- that the curtain must always fall on the hero or preferably the comic man in a attitude of triumph while the villain and villainess cower before he in baffled impotence we have perfect faith of course that the villain will come up smile in the next act and proceed with he nefarious practice but for the moment virtue have it all its own way this however be a very artless formulum which have somehow develop of recent year and it be doubtful whether even the audience to which these play appeal would not in reality prefer something a little less inept in the matter of construction as soon as we get above this level at all event the foster of anticipation become a matter of the first importance the problem be not to cut short the spectator 's interest or to leave it flutter at a loose end but to provide it either with a clearly-foreseen point in the next act towards which it can reach onward or with a definite enigma the solution of which be impatiently await in general term a bridge should be provide between one act and another along which the spectator 's mind can not but travel with eager anticipation and this be particularly important or particularly apt to be neglect at the end of the first act at a later point if the interest do not naturally and inevitably carry itself forward the case be hopeless indeed to illustrate what be mean by the carrying-forward of interest let I cite one or two instance in which it be achieve with conspicuous success in oscar wilde 's first modern comedy _ lady windermere 's fan _ the heroine lady windermere have learn that she husband have of late be see to call very frequently at the house of a certain mr erlynne whom nobody know she suspicion thus arouse she search she husband 's desk discover a private and lock bank-book cut it open and find that one large cheque after another have be draw in favor of the lady in question at this inopportune moment lord windermere appear with a request that mr erlynne shall be invite to they reception that evening lady windermere indignantly refuse she husband insist and finally with he own hand fill in a invitation-card and send it by messenger to mr erlynne here some playwright might have be content to finish the act it be sufficiently evident that lady windermere then he underline the definition in a perfectly natural and yet ingenious and skilful way the day happen to be lady windermere 's birthday and at the beginning of the act she husband have give she a pretty confident that he lordship 's conduct would scarcely justify itself but interest be largely independent of critical judgment and for my own part i can aver that when the curtain fall on the first act a five-pound note would not have bribe I to leave the theater without assist at lady windermere 's reception in the second act that be the frame of mind which the author should try to beget in he audience and oscar wilde then almost a novice have in this one little passage between lady windermere and the butler show himself a master of the art of dramatic story-telling the dramatist have obligatory scene i do not know whether it be francisque sarcey who invent the phrase _ scène à faire _ but it certainly owe its currency to that valiant champion of the theatrical theater if i may so express it note that in this term i intend no disrespect my conception of the theatrical theater may not be exactly the same as m sarcey 's but at all event i share he abhorrence of the untheatrical theater what be the _ scène à faire _ sarcey have use the phrase so often and in so many context that it be impossible to tie he down to any strict definition instead of try to do so i will give a typical example of the way in which he usually employ the term in _ les fourchambault _ by emile augier the first act introduce we to the household of a merchant of havre who have marry a wealthy but extravagant woman and have a son and daughter who be be gradually corrupt by they mother 's worldliness we learn that fourchambault senior have in he youth betray a young woman who be a governess in he family he want to marry she but he relation malign she character and he cast she off nor do he know what have become of she and she child in the second act we pass to the house of a energetic and successful young shipowner name bernard who live alone with he mother bernard as we divine be secretly devote to a young lady name marie letellier a guest in the fourchambault house to whom young leopold fourchambault be pay undesirable attention one day bernard casually mention to he mother that the house of fourchambault be on the verge of bankruptcy nothing less than a quarter of a million franc will enable it to tide over the crisis mme bernard to she son 's astonishment beg he to lend the totter firm the sum require he object that unless the business be better manage the loan will only postpone the inevitable disaster well then my son she reply you must go into partnership with m fourchambault i with that imbecile he exclaim my son she say gravely and emphatically you must -- it be you duty -- i demand it of you ah cry bernard i understand -- he be my father after ecstatically laud this situation and the scene which have lead up to it m sarcey continue -- when the curtain fall upon the word he be my father i at once see two _ scènes à faire _ and i know that they will be _ faite _ : the scene between the son and the father whom he be to save the scene between bernard and he half-brother leopold who be in love with the same woman the one dishonourably and the other secretly and nobly what will they say to each other i have no idea but it be precisely this _ expectation mingle with uncertainly _ that be one of the charm of the theater i say to myself ah they will have a encounter what will come of it and that this be the state of mind of the whole audience be prove by the fact that when the two character of the _ scènes à faire _ stand face to face a thrill of anticipation run round the whole theater this then be the obligatory scene as sarcey generally understand it -- a scene which for one reason or another a audience expect and ardently desire i have italicize the phrase expectation mingle with uncertainty because it express in other term the idea which i have seek to convey in the formulum foreshadowing without forestall but before we can judge of the merit of m sarcey 's theory we must look into it a little more closely i shall try then to state it in my own word in what i believe to be its most rational and defensible form a obligatory scene be one which the audience -lrb- more or less clearly and consciously -rrb- foresee and desire and the absence of which it may with reason resent on a rough analysis it will appear i think that there be five way in which a scene may become in this sense obligatory : -lrb- 1 -rrb- it may be necessitate by the inherent logic of the theme -lrb- 2 -rrb- it may be demand by the manifest exigency of specifically dramatic effect -lrb- 3 -rrb- the author himself may have render it obligatory by seem unmistakably to lead up to it -lrb- 4 -rrb- it may be require in order to justify some modification of character or alteration of will too important to be take for grant -lrb- 5 -rrb- it may be impose by history or legend these five class of obligatory scene may be docket respectively as the logical the dramatic the structural the psychological and the historic m sarcey generally employ the term in one of the first three sens without clearly distinguish between they it be indeed not always easy to determine whether the compulsion -lrb- assume it to exist at all -rrb- lie in the very essence of the theme or situation or only in the author 's manipulation of it be sarcey right in assume such a compulsion to be a constant and dominant factor in the playwright 's craft i think we shall see reason to believe he right in hold that it frequently arise but wrong if he go the length of maintain that there can be no good play without a definite _ scène à faire _ -- as eighteenth-century landscape painter be say to have hold that no one could be a master of he art till he know where to place the brown tree i remember no passage in which sarcey explicitly lay down so hard and fast a rule but several in which he seem to take it for grant -lrb- 1 -rrb- it may be ask whether -- and if so why -- the theory of the obligatory scene hold good for the dramatist and not for the novelist perhaps it have more application to the novel than be commonly suppose but in so far as it apply peculiarly to the drama the reason be pretty clear it lie in the strict concentration impose on the dramatist and the high mental tension which be or ought to be characteristic of the theatrical audience the leisurely and comparatively passive novel-reader may never miss a scene which a audience with its instinct of logic and of economy keenly alert may feel to be inevitable the dramatist be bind to extract from he material the last particle of that particular order of effect which the stage and the stage alone can give we if he fail to do so we feel that there have be no adequate justification for set in motion all the complex mechanism of the theater he play be like a badly-designed engine in which a large part of the potential energy be dissipate to no purpose the novelist with a far wider range of effect at he command and employ no special mechanism to bring they home to we be much more free to select and to reject he be exempt from the law of rigid economy to which the dramatist must submit far from be bind to do thing in the most dramatic way he often do wisely in reject that course as unsuited to he medium fundamentally no doubt the same principle apply to both art but with a wholly different stringency in the case of the drama advisable in the novelist 's vocabulary be translate by imperative in the dramatist 's the one be play a long-drawn game in which the loss of a trick or two need not prove fatal the other have stake he all on a single rubber \* \* \* \* \* obligatory scene of the first type -- those necessitate by the inherent logic of the theme -- can naturally arise only in play to which a definite theme can be assign if we say that woman 's claim to possess a soul of she own even in marriage be the theme of _ a doll 's house _ then evidently the last great balancing of account between nora and helmer be a obligatory scene it would have be quite possible for ibsen to have complete the play without any such scene : he might for instance have let nora fulfil she intention of drown herself but in that case he play would have be merely a tragic anecdote with the point omit we should have feel vague intimation of a general idea hover in the air but it would have remain undefined and undeveloped as we review however the series of ibsen 's play and notice how difficult it be to point to any individual scene and say this be clearly the _ scène à faire _ we feel that though the phrase may express a useful idea in a now maintain that accusation in _ john gabriel borhman _ if we define the theme as the clash of two devour egoism ibsen have in the third act give we the obligatory scene but he have do it tame and essentially selfish emotion absolutely powerless when it come into competition with the passion which be concern with the transmission of the vital flame this theorem have be state what be the first obligatory scene evidently one in which a mother shall refuse a second marriage with a man whom she love because it would injure the prospects and wind the feeling of she adored daughter then when the adore daughter herself marry the mother must make every possible sacrifice for she and the daughter must accept they all with indifference as mere matter of course but what be the final triumphant proof of the theorem why of course the mother must kill she mother to save the daughter 's life and this ultra-obligatory scene m hervieu duly serve up to we marie-jeanne -lrb- the daughter prefer to the improbable possible with all respect this seem to be a somewhat cumbrous way of state the fact that plausibility be of more importance on the stage than what may be call demonstrable probability there be no time in the rush of a dramatic action for a mathematical calculation of the chance for and against a give event or for experimental proof that such and such a thing can or can not be do if a thing seem plausible a audience will accept it without cavil if it seem incredible on the face of it no evidence of its credibility will be of much avail this be merely a corollary from the fundamental principle that the stage be the realm of appearance not of reality where paste jewel be at least as effective as real one and a paint forest be far more sylvan than a few wilted and drooping sapling insecurely plant upon the board that be why a improbable or otherwise inacceptable incident can not be validly defend on the plea that it actually happen : that it be on record in history or in the newspaper in the first place the dramatist can never put it on the stage as it happen the bare fact may be historical but it be not the bare fact that matter the dramatist can not restore it to its place in that intricate plexus of cause and effect which be the essence and meaning of reality he can only give he interpretation of the fact and one know not how to calculate the chance that he interpretation may be a false one but even if this difficulty could be overcome if the dramatist could prove that he have reproduce the event with photographic and cinematographic accuracy he position would not thereby be improve he would still have fail in he peculiar task which be precisely that of interpretation not truth but verisimilitude be he aim for the stage be the realm of appearance in which intrusive reality become unreal there be as i have say incalculable chance to one that the playwright 's version of a give event will not coincide with that of the recording angel : but it may be true and convincing in relation to human nature in general in which case it will belong to the sphere of great art or on a lower level it may be agreeable and entertaining without be conspicuously false to human nature in which case it will do no harm since it make no pretence to historic truth it may be object that the sixteenth-century public and even in the next century the great duke of marlborough get they knowledge of english history from shakespeare and the other writer of chronicle-play well i leave it to historian to determine whether this very defective and in great measure false vision of the past be better or worse than none the danger at any rate if danger there be be now past and do with even we general no longer go to the theater or to the first folio for they history the dramatist may with a easy conscience interpret historic fact in the light of he general insight into human nature so long as he do not so falsify the record event that common knowledge cry out against he -lrb- 1 -rrb- plausibility then not abstract or concrete probability and still less literal faithfulness to record fact be what the dramatist be bind to aim at to understand this as a belittle of he art be to misunderstand the nature of art in general the plausibility of bad art be doubtless contemptible and may be harmful but to say that good art must be plausible be only to say that not every sort of truth or every aspect of truth be equally suitable for artistic representation -- or in more general term that the artist without prejudice to he allegiance to nature must respect the condition of the medium in which he work we standard of plausibility however be far from be invariable to each separate form of art a different standard be applicable in what may roughly be call realistic art the term plausible and probable be very nearly interchangeable where the dramatist appeal to the sanction of we own experience and knowledge he must not introduce matter against which we experience and knowledge cry out a very small inaccuracy in a picture which be otherwise photographic will often have a very disturbing effect in play of society in particular the criticism no one do such thing be hold by a large class of playgoer to be conclusive and destructive one have know people despise a play because lady so-and-so 's manner of speak to she servant be not what they -lrb- the caviller -rrb- be accustom to on the other hand one have hear a whole production highly applaud because the button on a particular uniform be absolutely right this merely mean that when a effort after literal accuracy be apparent the attention of the audience seize on the most trifling detail and be apt to magnify they importance nicety of language in especial be keenly and often unjustly criticize if a particular expression do not happen to be current in the critic 's own circle he conclude that nobody use it and that the author be a pedant or a vulgarian in view of this inevitable tendency the prudent dramatist will try to keep out of he dialogue expression that be peculiar to he own circle and to use only what may be call everybody 's english or the language undoubtedly current throughout the whole class to which he personage belong it may be here point out that there be three different plane on which plausibility may or may not be achieve there be first the purely external plane which concern the producer almost as much as the playwright on this plane we look for plausibility of costume of manners of dialect of general environment then we have plausibility of what may be call uncharacteristic event -- of such event as be independent of the will of the character and be not condition by they psychology on this plane we have to deal with chance and accident coincidence and all circumstance over which we have no control for instance the playwright who make the marseillaise become popular throughout pari within half-an-hour of its have leave the composer 's desk be guilty of a breach of plausibility on this plane so too if i be to make my hero enter parliament for the first time and rise in a single session to be prime minister of england -- there would be no absolute impossibility in the feat but it would be a rather gross improbability of the second order on the third plane we come to psychological plausibility the plausibility of event dependent mainly or entirely on character for example -- to cite a much disputed instance -- be it plausible that nora in _ a doll 's house _ should suddenly develop the mastery of dialectic with which she crush helmer in the final scene and should desert she husband and child slam the door behind she it need scarcely be say that plausibility on the third plane be vastly the most important a very austere criticism might even call it the one thing worth consideration but as a matter of fact when we speak of plausibility it be almost always the second plane -- the plane of uncharacteristic circumstance -- that we have in mind to plausibility of the third order we give a more impose name -- we call it truth we say that nora 's action be true -- or untrue -- to nature we speak of the truth with which the madness of lear the malignity of iago the race hatred of shylock be portray truth in fact be the term which we use in case where the test to be apply be those of introspection intuition or knowledge sub-consciously garner from spiritual experience where the test be external and matter of common knowledge or tangible evidence we speak of plausibility it would be a mistake however to imagine that because plausibility of the third degree or truth be the noblest attribute of drama it be therefore the one thing needful in some form of drama it be greatly impaired or absolutely nullify if plausibility of the second degree its necessary preliminary be not carefully secure in the case above imagine for instance of the young politician who should become prime minister immediately on enter parliament : it would matter nothing with what profundity of knowledge or subtlety of skill the character be draw : we should none the less decline to believe in he some dramatist as a matter of fact find it much easier to attain truth of character than plausibility of incident every one who be in the habit of read manuscript play must have come across the would-be playwright who have a good deal of general ability and a considerable power of characterization but seem to be congenitally deficient in the sense of external reality so that the one thing he -lrb- or she -rrb- can by no means do be to invent or conduct a action that shall be in the least like any sequence of event in real life it be naturally difficult to give example for the play compose under this curious limitation be apt to remain in manuscript or to be produce for one performance and forget there be however one recent play of this order which hold a certain place in dramatic literature i do not know that mr granville barker be well-advised in print _ the marry of anne leete _ along with such immeasurably maturer and saner production as _ the degree so absolutely do it lack that plausibility of the second degree which be its indispensable condition precedent francisque sarcey be fond of insist that a audience would generally accept without cavil any postulate in reason which a author choose to impose upon it with regard to event suppose to have occur before the rise of the curtain always provide that the consequence deduce from they within the limit of the play be logical plausible and entertaining the public will swallow a camel he would maintain in the past though they will strain at a gnat in the present a classical example of development of a play from that point of view we all realize that a dramatist have no right to trust to we previous knowledge acquire from outside source we know that a play like every other work of
