and anticlimax if it be as easy to write a good last act as a good first act we should be able to reckon three masterpiece for every one that we can name at present the reason why the last act should offer special difficulty be not far to seek we have agree to regard a play as essentially a crisis in the life of one or more person and we all know that crisis be much more apt to have a definite beginning than a definite end we can almost always put we finger upon the moment -- not indeed when the crisis begin -- but when we clearly realize its presence or its imminence a chance meet the receipt of a letter or a telegram a particular turn give to a certain conversation even the mere emergence into consciousness of a previously latent feeling or thought may mark quite definitely the moment of germination so to speak of a give crisis and it be comparatively easy to dramatize such a moment but how few crisis come to a definite or dramatic conclusion nine time out of ten they end in some petty compromise or do not end at all but simply subside like the wave of the sea when the storm have blow itself out it be the playwright 's chief difficulty to find a crisis with a end which satisfy at once he artistic conscience and the requirement of dramatic effect and the difficulty become greater the nearer we approach to reality in the day when tragedy and comedy be cast in fixed conventional mould the playwright 's task be much simpler it be thoroughly understand that a tragedy end with one or more death a comedy with one or more marriage so that the question of a strong or a weak end do not arise the end might be strongly or weakly lead up to but in itself it be fore-ordained now that these mould be break and both marriage and death may be say to have lose they prestige as the be-all and end-all of drama the playwright 's range of choice be unlimited and the difficulty of choosing have become infinitely greater we comedy be much more apt to begin than to end with marriage and death have come to be regard as a rather cheap and conventional expedient for cut the knot of life from the fact that the difficulty become greater the nearer we approach to reality it further follow that the higher the form of drama the more probable be it that the demand of truth and the requirement of dramatic effect may be find to clash in melodrama the curtain fall of its own accord so to speak when the handcuffs be transfer from the hero 's wrist to the villain 's in a adventure-play whether farcical or romantic when the adventure be over the play be do the author 's task be merely to keep the interest of the adventure afoot until he be ready to drop he curtain this be a point of craftsmanship in which playwright often fail but it be a point of craftsmanship only in play of a higher order on the other hand the difficulty be often inherent in the theme and not to be overcome by any feat of craftsmanship if the dramatist be to eschew all crisis that could not be make to resolve themselves with specifically dramatic crispness and decisiveness he would very seriously limit the domain of he art many excellent theme would be distorted and ruin by have a emphatic end force upon they it be surely much better that they should be bring to they natural unemphatic ending than that they should be either falsify or ignore i suggest then that the modern tendency to take lightly aristotle 's demand that the drama should have a begin a middle _ and a end _ arise from the nature of thing and imply not necessarily nor even probably a decline in craftsmanship but a new intimacy of relation to life and a new sincerity of artistic conscience i suggest that the weak last act of which critic so often complain be a natural development from which author ought not on occasion to shrink and of which critic ought on occasion to recognize the necessity to elevate it into a system be absurd there be certainly no more reason for deliberately avoid a emphatic ending than for mechanically force one but author and critic alike should learn to distinguish the theme which do from the theme which do not call for a definite trenchant solution and should handle they and judge they in accordance with they inherent quality let we however define we term and be sure that we know what we be talk about by a unemphatic end i be far from mean a makeshift end a end carelessly elegiac or philosophic last act follow upon a penultimate act of very much higher tension the disposition to condemn such a end off-hand be what i be here plead against it be sometimes assume that the playwright ought always to make he action conclude within five minute of its culmination but for such a hard-and-fast rule i can find no sufficient reason the consequence of a great emotional or theme -- and other a blind-alley theme as its name import be one from which there be no exit it be a problem incapable of solution or rather of which all possible solution be equally unsatisfactory and undesirable the playwright can not too soon make sure that he have not stray into such a no-thoroughfare whether a end be comic or tragic romantic or ironic happy or disastrous it should satisfy something within we -- we sense of truth or of beauty or of sublimity or of justice or of humor or at the least or lowest we cynical sense of the baseness of human nature and the vanity of human aspiration but a play which satisfy neither we higher nor we lower instinct baffle we sympathy and leave we desire at fault between equally inacceptable alternative -- such a play whatever beauty of detail it may possess be a weariness of the spirit and a artistic blunder there be in literature two conspicuous example of the blind-alley theme -- two famous play wherein two heroine be place in somewhat similar dilemma which merely paralyse we sympathy and inhibit we moral judgment the first of these be _ measure for measure _ if ever there be a insoluble problem in casuistry it be that which shakespeare have here choose to present to we isabellum be force to choose between what we can only describe as two detestable evil if she resist angelo and let she brother die she recoil from a \* what be to be say of the possibility of blank verse as a dramatic medium this be a thorny question to be handle with caution one can say with perfect assurance however that its possibility be problematical its difficulty and danger certain to discuss the question whether drama in verse be in its very nature nobler than drama in prose would lead we away from craftsmanship into the realm of pure aesthetics for my own part i doubt it i suspect that the drama like all literature take its rise in verse for the simple reason that verse be easier to make -- and to memorize -- than prose primitive people feel with goethe -- though not quite in the same sense -- that art be art because it be not nature not merely for emotional but for all sort of literary expression they demand a mark off by the proscenium arch and the artificial light which make a world apart of the modern stage and shakespeare so glorified this metrical medium as to give it a overwhelming prestige it be extremely easy to write blank verse after a fashion and playwright who find it flow almost spontaneously from they pen be only too ready to overlook the world-wide difference between they verse and that of the really great elizabethan just after the restoration there be a attempt to introduce the rhymed couplet as the medium for heroic play but that on the other hand be too difficult to establish itself in general use tragedy soon fall back upon the fatally facile unrhymed iambic and a reign of stilted stodgy mediocrity set in there be nothing drearier in literature than the century-and-a-half of english tragedy from otway to sheridan knowle one be lose in wonder at the genius of the actor who could infuse life and passion into those masterpiece of turgid conventionality the worship of the minor elizabethan which begin with lamb and culminate in swinburne bring into fashion -lrb- as we have see -rrb- a spasmodic rather than a smoothly rhetorical way of write but do not really put new life into the outworn form it may almost be call a appalling fact that for at least two century -- from 1700 to 1900 -- not a single blank-verse play be produce which live or deserve to live -lrb- 2 -rrb- on the stage of to-day i have thus glance at the history of the blank-verse play because i believe that it can never revive until we clearly realize and admit that it be and have be for a century thoroughly dead while for a century before that again it be only galvanized into a semblance of life by a great school of rhetorical act the playwright who set forth with the idea that in write a poetical drama he be go to continue the great elizabethan tradition be start on a wild-goose chase the great elizabethan tradition be a incubus to be exorcise it be because mr verse-passage a air of pompous self-consciousness we seem to hear the author say as he shift he gear look you now i be go to be eloquent and impressive the most destructive fault a dramatist can commit in my judgment be to pass in the same work of art from one plane of convention to another -lrb- 3 -rrb- \* \* \* \* \* we must now consider for a moment the question -- if question it can be no doubt there be a irreducible minimum of convention in all drama but how strange be the logic which leap from that postulate to the assertion that if we admit a minimum we can not or ought not to exclude a maximum there be play which do not and there be play which do set forth to give as nearly as possible a exact reproduction of the visual and auditory reality of life in the elizabethan therefore such convention as blank verse and the soliloquy be simply of a piece with all the rest in the theater of the eighteenth century and early nineteenth the proscenium arch -- the frame of the platform survive in the shape of a long apron project in front of the proscenium on which the most important part of the action take place the character that be to say be constantly step out of the frame of the picture and while this visual convention maintain itself there be nothing inconsistent or jarring in the auditory convention of the soliloquy only in the last quarter of the nineteenth century do new method of lighting combine with new literary and artistic influence complete the evolutionary process and lead to the withdrawal of the whole stage -- the whole dramatic domain -- within the frame of the picture it be thus possible to reduce visual convention to a minimum so trifling that in a well-set interior it need a distinct effort of attention to be conscious of it at all in fact if we come to think of it the removal of the fourth wall be scarcely to be class as a convention for in real life as we do not happen to have eye in the back of we head we be never visually conscious of all four wall of a room at once if then in a room that be absolutely real we see a man who -lrb- in all other respects -rrb- strive to be equally real suddenly begin to expound himself aloud in good set term he own emotion motive or purpose we instantly plump down from one plane of convention to another and receive a disagreeable jar to we sense of reality up to that moment all the effort of author producer and actor have centre in beget in we a particular order of illusion and lo the effort be suddenly abandon and the illusion shatter by a cry unreality in modern serious drama therefore the soliloquy can only be regard as a disturbing anachronism -lrb- 5 -rrb- the physical condition which tend to banish it from the stage be reinforce by the grow perception of its artistic slovenliness it be find that the most delicate analysis could be achieve without its aid and it become a point of honor with the self-respecting artist to accept a condition which render he material somewhat harder of manipulation indeed but all the more tempting to wrestle with and overcome a drama with soliloquy and aside be like a picture with inscribed label issue from the mouth of the figure in that way any bungler can reveal what be pass in the mind of he personage but the glorious problem of the modern playwright be to make he character reveal the inmost workings of they soul without say or do anything that they would not say or do in the real world -lrb- 6 -rrb- there be degree however even in the makeshift and the slovenly and not all lapse into anachronism be equally to be condemn one thing be so patent as to call for no demonstration : to wit that the aside be ten time worse than the soliloquy it be always possible that a man might speak he thought but it be glaringly impossible that he should speak it so as to be hear by the audience and not hear by other on the stage in french light comedy and farce of the mid-nineteenth century the aside be abused beyond even the license of fantasy a man will speak a aside of several line over the shoulder of another person whom he be embrace not infrequently in a conversation between two character each will comment aside on every utterance of the other before reply to it the convenience of this method of proceeding be manifest it be as though the author stand by and deliver a run commentary on the secret motive and design of he character but it be such a cry confession of unreality that on the english-speaking stage at any rate it would scarcely be tolerate to-day even in farce in serious modern drama the aside it be _ not _ hear by the other person on the stage : it outrage physical possibility a overheard soliloquy on the other hand be intolerable because it _ be _ hear it keep within the bound of physical possibility but it stultify the only logical excuse for the soliloquy namely that it be a externalization of thought which would in reality remain unuttered this point be so clear that i need not insist upon it be there in modern drama any admissible soliloquy a few brief ejaculation of joy or despair be of course natural enough and no one will cavil at they the approach of mental disease be often mark by a tendency to unrestrained loquacity which go on even while the sufferer be alone and this distressing symptom may on rare occasion be put to artistic use short of actual derangement crisis he may risk a soliloquy not only without reproach but with conspicuous psychological justification in the third act of clyde fitch 's play _ the girl with the green eye _ there be a daring whether the attempt justified itself or not would depend largely on the act in any case it be clear that the author though as a rule somewhat lax in he craftsmanship be here aim at psychological truth a word must be say as to a special case of the soliloquy -- the letter which a person speak aloud as he write it or read over to himself aloud this be a convention to be employ as sparingly as possible but it be not exactly on a level with the ordinary soliloquy a letter have a actual objective existence the word be formulate in the character 's mind and be suppose to be externalize even though the actor may not really write they on the paper thus the letter have so to speak the same right to come to the knowledge of the audience as any other utterance it be in fact part of the dialogue of the play only that it happen to be inaudible a soliloquy on the other hand have no real existence it be a purely artificial unravelling of motive or emotion which nine time out of ten would not become articulate at all even in the speaker 's brain or heart thus it be by many degree a greater infraction of the surface texture of life than the speak letter which we may call inadvisable rather than inadmissible some theorist carry they solicitude for surface reality to such a extreme as to object to any communication between two character which be not audible to every one on the stage this be a very idle pedantry the difference between a conversation in undertone and a soliloquy or aside be abundantly plain : the one occur every hour of the day the other never occur at all when two people or a group be talk among themselves unheard by the other on the stage it require a special effort to remember that as a matter of fact the other probably do hear they even if the scene be unskilfully arrange it be not the audibility of one group but the inaudibility of the other that be apt to strike we as unreal \* \* \* \* \* this be not the only form of technical pedantry that one occasionally encounter some year ago a little band of playwright and would-be playwright in fanatical reaction against the sardou technique try to lay down a rule that no room on the stage must ever have more than one door and that no letter must ever enter into the mechanism of a play i do not know which contention be the more ridiculous nothing be commoner in modern house-planning than room which have at least two door and a french window we constantly see room or hall which if transport to the stage would provide three or four entrance and exit and this be even more true of the central heated house of america than of english house the technical purist use especially to despise the french window -- a harmless agreeable and very common device why the playwright should make one room one door a inexorable canon of art be more than human reason can divine there be case no doubt in which probability demand that the dramatist should be content with one practicable open to he scene and should plan he entrance and exit accordingly this be no such great feat as might be imagine indeed a playwright will sometimes deliberately place a particular act in a room with one door because it happen to facilitate the movement he desire it be absurd to lay down any rule in the matter other than that the scene should provide a probable locality for whatever action be to take place in it i be the last to defend the old french farce with its ten or a dozen door through which the character keep scuttle in and out like rabbit in a warren but the fact that we be tire of conventional laxity be no good reason for rush to the other extreme of conventional and hamper austerity similarly because the forge will and the lose marriage line have be rightly relegate to melodrama be there any reason why we should banish from the stage every form of write document mr bernard shaw in a article celebrate the advent of the new technique once write nowadays a actor can not open a letter or toss off somebody else 's glass of poison without have to face a brutal outburst of jeer what a extravagance to bracket as equally explode absurdity the opening of a letter and the toss off of the wrong glass of poison letter -- more 's the pity -- play a gigantic part in the economy of modern life the general post office be a vast mechanism for the distribution of tragedy comedy melodrama and farce throughout the country and throughout the world to whose door have not destiny come in the disguise of a postman and slip its decree with a double rat-tat into the letter-box whose heart have not sicken as he hear the postman 's footstep pass he door without pause whose hand have not tremble as he open a letter whose face have not blanch as he take in its import almost without read the word why i would fain know should we stage-picture of life be falsify by the banishment of the postman even the revelation bring about by the discovery of a forget letter or bundle of letter be not a sour grape and other people 's child must pay the penalty against such whimsy of reactionary purism the playwright 's sole and sufficient safeguard be a moderate exercise of common sense \* \* \* \*
